      <p><b>John Donald ("Don" or "Donnie") Budge</b> (June 13, 1915 – January 26, 2000) was an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a> player. He is most famous as the first player — of any nationality, male or female — and only American male to win the four tournaments that comprise the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Slam_in_tennis" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Slam in tennis">Grand Slam</a> of tennis in a single year.<sup id="cite_ref-espn_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-espn-3">[3]</a></sup> Budge was the second male player to win all four Grand Slam events in his career after <a href="/wiki/Fred_Perry" title="Fred Perry">Fred Perry</a>, and is still the youngest to achieve that feat. He won 10 majors, of which six were <a href="/wiki/Grand_Slam_(tennis)" title="Grand Slam (tennis)">Grand Slam events</a> (consecutively, male record) and four <a href="/wiki/Major_professional_tennis_tournaments_before_the_Open_Era" title="Major professional tennis tournaments before the Open Era">Pro Slams</a>, the latter achieved on three different surfaces. Budge was considered to have the best <a href="/wiki/Backhand" title="Backhand">backhand</a> in the history of tennis, at least until the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Ken_Rosewall" title="Ken Rosewall">Ken Rosewall</a> in the 1950s and 1960s, although most observers rated Budge's backhand the stronger of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-drucker_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drucker-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Budge was born in <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland, California</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> immigrant and former <a href="/wiki/Soccer" class="mw-redirect" title="Soccer">soccer</a> player John "Jack" Budge, his father had played several matches for the <a href="/wiki/Rangers_F.C." title="Rangers F.C.">Rangers</a> <a href="/wiki/Reserve_team" title="Reserve team">reserve team</a> before <a href="/wiki/Emigrating" class="mw-redirect" title="Emigrating">emigrating</a> to the United States, and Pearl Kincaid Budge.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Growing up, he played a variety of sports before taking up tennis. He was tall and slim and his height would later help what is still considered one of the most powerful serves of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-7">[7]</a></sup> Budge studied at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a> in late 1933 but left to play tennis with the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Davis_Cup" title="Davis Cup">Davis Cup</a> auxiliary team. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur_career">Amateur career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Amateur career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Accustomed to hard-court surfaces in his native California, he had difficulty playing on the <a href="/wiki/Grass_court" title="Grass court">grass</a> surfaces in the east. However, a good instructor and hard work changed that, and in both 1937 and 1938 he swept <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Wimbledon Championships">Wimbledon</a>, winning the singles, the men's doubles title with <a href="/wiki/Gene_Mako" title="Gene Mako">Gene Mako</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_doubles_(tennis)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed doubles (tennis)">mixed doubles</a> crown with <a href="/wiki/Alice_Marble" title="Alice Marble">Alice Marble</a>, a feat which he repeated at the <a href="/wiki/1938_U.S._National_Championships_(tennis)" title="1938 U.S. National Championships (tennis)">1938 US Championships</a>. Budge became the first man in history to have achieved the "Triple Crown" at a Grand Slam event three times, eclipsing <a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilden" title="Bill Tilden">Bill Tilden</a> who won consecutive Triple Crowns at the U.S. Championships. </p>
      <p>He gained the most fame for his match that year against <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_von_Cramm" title="Gottfried von Cramm">Gottfried von Cramm</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Davis_Cup" title="Davis Cup">Davis Cup</a> inter-zone finals against <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. Trailing 1–4 in the final set, he came back to win 8–6. His victory allowed the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> to advance and to then win the Davis Cup for the first time in 12 years. For his efforts, he was named <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Male_Athlete_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Associated Press Male Athlete of the Year">Associated Press Male Athlete of the Year</a> and he became the first tennis player ever to be voted the <a href="/wiki/James_E._Sullivan_Award" title="James E. Sullivan Award">James E. Sullivan Award</a> as America's top amateur athlete. </p>
      <p>In 1938 Budge dominated amateur tennis, defeating <a href="/wiki/John_Bromwich" title="John Bromwich">John Bromwich</a> in the Australian final, <a href="/wiki/Roderick_Menzel" class="mw-redirect" title="Roderick Menzel">Roderick Menzel</a> in the French final, <a href="/wiki/Bunny_Austin" title="Bunny Austin">Henry "Bunny" Austin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Wimbledon Championships">Wimbledon</a>, where he never lost a set, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Mako" title="Gene Mako">Gene Mako</a> in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=US_(tennis)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="US (tennis) (page does not exist)">U.S.</a> final, to become the first person ever to win the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Slam_in_tennis" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Slam in tennis">Grand Slam in tennis</a>. He also is the youngest man in history to complete the career Grand Slam (the four majors in one's career). He completed that on June 11, 1938 in winning the French singles, two days before his 23rd birthday. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_career">Professional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Professional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Budge turned professional in October 1938, after winning the Grand Slam, and thereafter played mostly head-to-head matches. In 1939 he beat the two reigning kings of professional tennis, <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Vines" title="Ellsworth Vines">Ellsworth Vines</a>, 22 matches to 17, and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Perry" title="Fred Perry">Fred Perry</a>, 28 matches to 8.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> That year he also won two major pro tournaments, the <a href="/wiki/Professional_Tennis_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional Tennis Championships">French Pro Championship</a> over Vines and the <a href="/wiki/Wembley_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="Wembley Championship">Wembley</a> Pro tournament over <a href="/wiki/Hans_N%C3%BCsslein" title="Hans Nüsslein">Hans Nüsslein</a>. There was no professional tour in 1940 but seven principal tournaments. Budge kept his world crown by winning 4 of these events including the greatest one, the <a href="/wiki/Professional_Tennis_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional Tennis Championships">United States Pro Championship</a>. In 1941 Budge played another major tour beating the 48-year-old <a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilden" title="Bill Tilden">Bill Tilden</a>, the final outcome being 46–7 plus 1 tie. In 1942 Budge won both his last major tour over <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Riggs" title="Bobby Riggs">Bobby Riggs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kovacs" title="Frank Kovacs">Frank Kovacs</a>, Perry and <a href="/wiki/Les_Stoefen" class="mw-redirect" title="Les Stoefen">Les Stoefen</a> and for a second time the U.S. Pro, crushing Riggs 6–2, 6–2, 6–2 in the final. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_service">Military service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Military service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Don_Budge_1937.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Don_Budge_1937.jpg/220px-Don_Budge_1937.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Don_Budge_1937.jpg/330px-Don_Budge_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Don_Budge_1937.jpg/440px-Don_Budge_1937.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1355" data-file-height="1042"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Don_Budge_1937.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Don Budge at the 
         <a href="/wiki/White_City_Stadium_(Sydney)" title="White City Stadium (Sydney)">White City Stadium</a>, Sydney in December 1937
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1942 Budge joined the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Army Air Force</a> to serve in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. At the beginning of 1943 in an obstacle course he tore a muscle in his shoulder. In his book 'A Tennis Memoir' page 144 he said: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>The tear didn't heal, and the scar tissue that was formed complicated the injury and made it even serious. Nevertheless ... I was able to carry on with my military duties ... as long as two years afterwards, in the spring of '45, I was given a full month's medical leave so that I could go to Berkeley and have an osteopath, Dr. J. LeRoy Near, work with me. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>This permanently hindered his playing abilities. During his wartime duty he played some exhibitions for the troops in particular during the summer 1945 with the war winding down, Budge played in a U.S Army <i>(Budge-<a href="/wiki/Frank_Parker" title="Frank Parker">Frank Parker</a>)</i> – U.S. Navy <i>(Riggs – <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Sabin" title="Wayne Sabin">Wayne Sabin</a>)</i> competition under the Davis Cup format&nbsp;: the main confrontations were the Budge-Riggs meetings knowing that both Americans were the best players in the world in 1942 just before being enlisted in the U.S. Armed Forces and again when they came back to the professional circuit in 1945. In the first match, on the island of Guam, Budge trounced Riggs 6–2, 6–2. On the island of Peleliu Budge won again 6–4, 7–5. Riggs won the next two matches against Budge 6–1, 6–1 (island of Ulithi) and 6–3, 4–6, 6–1 (island of Saipan). Budge confided in Parker his disbelief at losing two matches in a row to Riggs. In the fifth and final match on the island of Tinian, scheduled for the first week of August 1945, Riggs defeated Budge 6–8 6–1 8–6. This was the first time Budge had been beaten by Riggs in a series (Riggs also won 3 matches out of 5 against the amateur Parker, both holder and future titlist of the U.S. amateur Nationals at Forest Hills) thereby giving Riggs an important psychological edge in their forthcoming peacetime tours.<sup id="cite_ref-racketBook_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racketBook-11">[11]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_war">Post war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Post war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>After the war Budge played for a few years, mostly against Riggs. In 1946 Budge lost narrowly to Riggs in their U.S. tour, 24 matches to 22. The hierarchy was confirmed at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Professional_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Professional Championship">U.S. Pro</a>, held at <a href="/wiki/West_Side_Tennis_Club" title="West Side Tennis Club">Forest Hills</a> where Riggs easily defeated Budge in the last round. Next year Riggs stayed the pro king by defeating again Budge in the U.S. Pro final in five sets. Riggs then established himself as the <a href="/wiki/World_number_one_male_tennis_player_rankings" class="mw-redirect" title="World number one male tennis player rankings">World No. 1</a> for those two years. According to Kramer, </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036">
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Bobby played to Budge's shoulder, lobbed him to death, won the first twelve matches, thirteen out of the first fourteen, and then hung on to beat Budge, twenty-four matches to twenty-two. At the age of thirty Don Budge was very nearly a has-been. That was the way pro tennis worked then. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>According to Riggs, however, Budge still had a very powerful, very deadly overhead and rather than winning outright very many points with his lobbing, he actually achieved two other goals: his constant lobbing led Budge to play somewhat deeper at the net than he would have otherwise, thereby making it easier for Riggs to hit passing shots for winners; and the constant lobbing helped to wear Budge down by forcing him to run back to the backline time after time.<sup id="cite_ref-racketBook_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racketBook-11">[11]</a></sup> Budge reached two more U.S. Pro finals, losing in 1949 at Forest Hills to Riggs and in 1953 in Cleveland to Pancho Gonzales. </p>
      <p>In 1954 Budge recorded his last significant victory in a North American tour with <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Gonzales" title="Pancho Gonzales">Pancho Gonzales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Segura" title="Pancho Segura">Pancho Segura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sedgman" title="Frank Sedgman">Frank Sedgman</a> when, in Los Angeles, he defeated Gonzales, by then the best player in the world. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_and_honors">Later years and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Later years and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>After retiring from competition Budge coached and conducted tennis clinics for children. According to Riggs' 1949 autobiography as of that writing, Budge owned a laundry in New York with <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Wood" title="Sidney Wood">Sidney Wood</a> as well as a bar in Oakland. A gentleman on and off the court, he was much in demand for speaking engagements and endorsed various lines of sporting goods. With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Tennis_Open_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Tennis Open Era">Open era</a> in tennis, in 1968 he returned to play at Wimbledon in the Veteran's doubles. In 1973, at the age of 58, he and former champion <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sedgman" title="Frank Sedgman">Frank Sedgman</a> teamed up to win the Veteran's doubles championship at Wimbledon before an appreciative crowd. </p>
      <p>Budge was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/International_Tennis_Hall_of_Fame" title="International Tennis Hall of Fame">International Tennis Hall of Fame</a> at <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island" title="Newport, Rhode Island">Newport, Rhode Island</a> in 1964. Budge is referenced in the 1977 Broadway musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Annie_(musical)" title="Annie (musical)">Annie</a></i>, in the song "I Think I'm Gonna Like It Here." The reference is technically an anachronism, as the story is set in 1933, at which time Budge was an undergraduate at Berkeley and had not yet achieved prominence. The tennis courts at <a href="/wiki/Bushrod_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Bushrod Park">Bushrod Park</a> in north Oakland are named for Budge where he played as a youth. </p>
      <p>In December 1999, Budge was injured in an automobile accident from which he never fully recovered. He died on January 26, 2000 at a nursing home in <a href="/wiki/Scranton,_Pennsylvania" title="Scranton, Pennsylvania">Scranton, Pennsylvania</a>, aged 84. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessment">Assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Budge is a consensus pick for being one of the greatest players of all time. He had a graceful, overpowering backhand that he hit with a slight amount of topspin and that, combined with his quickness and his serve, made him the best player of his time. E. Digby Baltzell wrote in 1994 that Budge and Laver "have usually been rated at the top of any all-time World Champions list, Budge having a slight edge."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Will Grimsley wrote in 1971 that Budge "is considered by many to be foremost among the all-time greats." <sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Paul Metzler, in his analysis of ten of the all-time greats, singles out Budge as the greatest player before World War II, and gives him second place overall behind <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kramer_(tennis_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Kramer (tennis player)">Jack Kramer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Jack Kramer himself has written that Budge was, in the long run, the greatest player who ever lived although <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Vines" title="Ellsworth Vines">Ellsworth Vines</a> topped him when at the height of his game.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Kramer said: </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036">
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Budge was the best of all. He owned the most perfect set of mechanics and he was the most consistent.... Don was so good that when he toured with <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sedgman" title="Frank Sedgman">Sedgman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Gonzales" title="Pancho Gonzales">Gonzales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Segura" title="Pancho Segura">Segura</a> in 1954 at the age of thirty-eight, none of those guys could get to the net consistently off his serve—and Sedgman, as quick a man who ever played the game, was in his absolute prime then. Don could keep them pinned to the baseline with his backhand too. </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>In his 1979 autobiography Kramer considered the best player ever to have been either Don Budge (for consistent play) or <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Vines" title="Ellsworth Vines">Ellsworth Vines</a> (at the height of his game). The next four best were, chronologically, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilden" title="Bill Tilden">Bill Tilden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Perry" title="Fred Perry">Fred Perry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Riggs" title="Bobby Riggs">Bobby Riggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Gonzales" title="Pancho Gonzales">Pancho Gonzales</a>. All of these sources were written after <a href="/wiki/Rod_Laver" title="Rod Laver">Rod Laver</a> completed his second, and Open, Grand Slam in 1969. </p>
      <p>In early 1986 <a href="/wiki/Inside_Tennis" title="Inside Tennis">Inside Tennis</a>, a magazine edited in <a href="/wiki/Northern_California" title="Northern California">Northern California</a>, devoted parts of four issues to a lengthy article called "Tournament of the Century", an imaginary tournament to determine the greatest of all time. Twenty-five players in all were named by the 37 experts in their lists of the 10 best. The magazine then ranked them in descending order by total number of points assigned. The top eight players in overall points, with their number of first-place votes, were: <a href="/wiki/Rod_Laver" title="Rod Laver">Rod Laver</a> (9), <a href="/wiki/John_McEnroe" title="John McEnroe">John McEnroe</a> (3), Don Budge (4), <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kramer" title="Jack Kramer">Jack Kramer</a> (5), <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rn_Borg" title="Björn Borg">Björn Borg</a> (6), <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Gonzales" title="Pancho Gonzales">Pancho Gonzales</a> (1), <a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilden" title="Bill Tilden">Bill Tilden</a> (6), and <a href="/wiki/Lew_Hoad" title="Lew Hoad">Lew Hoad</a> (1). McEnroe was still an active player and Laver and Borg had only recently retired. In the imaginary tournament Laver beat McEnroe in the finals in 5 sets. </p>
      <p>More recently, an <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> poll conducted in 1999 ranked Budge fifth, following Laver, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Sampras" title="Pete Sampras">Pete Sampras</a>, Tilden, and Borg. Even more recently, in 2006, a panel of former players and experts was asked by <a href="/w/index.php?title=TennisWeek&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="TennisWeek (page does not exist)">TennisWeek</a> to assemble a draw for a fantasy tournament to determine who was the greatest of all time. The top eight seeds were <a href="/wiki/Roger_Federer" title="Roger Federer">Roger Federer</a>, Laver, Sampras, Borg, Tilden, Budge, Kramer, and McEnroe. In important polls, then, Budge has consistently been ranked in the top five or six. Perhaps only Tilden and Laver can boast such a high and long-standing critical assessment. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_finals">Major finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Major finals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grand_Slam_tournaments">Grand Slam tournaments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Grand Slam tournaments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span id="Singles:_7_.286_titles.2C_1_runner-up.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Singles:_7_(6_titles,_1_runner-up)">Singles: 7 (6 titles, 1 runner-up)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Singles: 7 (6 titles, 1 runner-up)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_timeline">Performance timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Performance timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Records">Records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Budge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

        <li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grimsley, Will: <i>Tennis: Its History, People and Events</i></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Metzler, Paul: <i>Tennis Styles and Stylists</i></span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his 1979 autobiography Kramer considered the best player ever to have been either Don Budge (for consistent play) or <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Vines" title="Ellsworth Vines">Ellsworth Vines</a> (at the height of his game). The next four best were, chronologically, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilden" title="Bill Tilden">Bill Tilden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fred_Perry" title="Fred Perry">Fred Perry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Riggs" title="Bobby Riggs">Bobby Riggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Gonzales" title="Pancho Gonzales">Pancho Gonzales</a>. After these six came the "second echelon" of <a href="/wiki/Rod_Laver" title="Rod Laver">Rod Laver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lew_Hoad" title="Lew Hoad">Lew Hoad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Rosewall" title="Ken Rosewall">Ken Rosewall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_von_Cramm" title="Gottfried von Cramm">Gottfried von Cramm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Schroeder" title="Ted Schroeder">Ted Schroeder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Crawford_(tennis_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Crawford (tennis player)">Jack Crawford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Segura" title="Pancho Segura">Pancho Segura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sedgman" title="Frank Sedgman">Frank Sedgman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Trabert" title="Tony Trabert">Tony Trabert</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Newcombe" title="John Newcombe">John Newcombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Ashe" title="Arthur Ashe">Arthur Ashe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Smith" title="Stan Smith">Stan Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rn_Borg" title="Björn Borg">Björn Borg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Connors" title="Jimmy Connors">Jimmy Connors</a>. He felt unable to rank <a href="/wiki/Henri_Cochet" title="Henri Cochet">Henri Cochet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Lacoste" title="René Lacoste">René Lacoste</a> accurately but felt they were among the very best.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Finn, Robin (January 27, 2000). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2000/01/27/sports/don-budge-first-to-win-tennis-s-grand-slam-dies-at-84.html">"Don Budge, First to Win Tennis's Grand Slam, Dies at 84"</a>. <i>www.nytimes.com</i>. <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
